<p><b>Andrew Jackson</b> (March 15, 1767&#160;– June 8, 1845) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">seventh</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1829–1837). He was born near the end of the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">colonial era</a>, somewhere near the then-unmarked border between <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, into a recently immigrated <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> farming family of relatively modest means. During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> Jackson, whose family supported the revolutionary cause, acted as a courier. He was captured, at age 13, and mistreated by his British captors. He later became a lawyer. He was elected to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives" class="mw-redirect">U.S. House of Representatives</a>, and then to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Senate</a>. In 1801, Jackson was appointed colonel in the Tennessee militia, which became his political as well as military base. Jackson owned hundreds of slaves who worked on the <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">Hermitage plantation</a> which he acquired in 1804. Jackson killed a man in a duel in 1806, over a matter of honor regarding his wife <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel</a>. Jackson gained national fame through his role in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, most famously where he won a decisive victory over the main British invasion army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a>. Jackson's army was then sent to Florida where he deposed the small Spanish garrison. This led directly to <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">the treaty</a> which formally transferred Florida from Spain to the United States.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Election_of_1824" title="Election of 1824" class="mw-redirect">Nominated for president in 1824</a>, Jackson narrowly lost to <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>. Jackson's supporters then founded what became the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">Democratic Party</a>. <a href="/wiki/Election_of_1828" title="Election of 1828" class="mw-redirect">Nominated again in 1828</a>, Jackson crusaded against Adams and the "corrupt bargain" between Adams and Henry Clay he said cost him the 1824 election. Building on his base in the West and new support from Virginia and New York, he won by a landslide. The Adams campaigners called him and his wife <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel Jackson</a> "bigamists"; she died just after the election and he called the slanderers "murderers," swearing never to forgive them. His struggles with Congress were personified in his personal rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, whom Jackson deeply disliked, and who led the opposition (the emerging <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a>). As president, he faced a threat of secession from <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> over the "<a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_Abominations" title="Tariff of Abominations">Tariff of Abominations</a>" which Congress had enacted under Adams. In contrast to several of his immediate successors, he denied the right of a state to secede from the union, or to nullify federal law. The <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a> was defused when the tariff was amended and Jackson <a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">threatened</a> the use of military force if South Carolina (or any other state) attempted to secede.</p>
<p>Congress attempted to reauthorize the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a> several years before the expiration of its charter, which he opposed. He vetoed the renewal of its charter in 1832, and dismantled it by the time its charter expired in 1836. Jackson's presidency marked the beginning of the ascendancy of the "<a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>" in American politics. Also, he supported, signed, and enforced the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>, which relocated a number of native tribes to <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>). He faced and defeated Henry Clay in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">1832 Presidential Election</a>, and opposed Clay generally. Jackson supported his vice president <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, who was elected president in 1836. He worked to bolster the Democratic Party and helped his friend <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> win the 1844 presidential election.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>
<p>Jackson was born on March 15, 1767. His parents were <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> colonists Andrew and Elizabeth Hutchinson Jackson, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect">Presbyterians</a> who had emigrated from <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> two years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-birthplace_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birthplace-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrick_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrick-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's father was born in <a href="/wiki/Carrickfergus" title="Carrickfergus">Carrickfergus</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a>, in current-day <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, around 1738.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's parents lived in the village of <a href="/wiki/Boneybefore" title="Boneybefore">Boneybefore</a>, also in County Antrim.</p>
<p>When they emigrated to America in 1765, Jackson's parents probably landed in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania. They would have traveled overland down through the Appalachian Mountains to the Scots-Irish community in the <a href="/wiki/Waxhaws" title="Waxhaws">Waxhaws</a> region, straddling the border between <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> They brought two children from Ireland, Hugh (born 1763) and Robert (born 1764).</p>
<p>Jackson's father died in an accident in February 1767, at the age of 29, three weeks before his son Andrew was born in the <a href="/wiki/Waxhaws" title="Waxhaws">Waxhaws</a> area. His exact birth site is unclear because he was born about the time his mother was making a difficult trip home from burying Jackson's father. The area was so remote that the border between North and South Carolina had not been officially surveyed.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1824, Jackson wrote a letter saying that he was born at an uncle's plantation in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>. But he may have claimed to be a South Carolinian because the state was considering nullification of the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1824" title="Tariff of 1824">Tariff of 1824</a>, which Jackson opposed. In the mid-1850s, second-hand evidence indicated that he may have been born at a different uncle's home in North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-Old_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson received a sporadic education in the local "old-field" school.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1781, he worked for a time in a saddle-maker's shop.<sup id="cite_ref-Paletta_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paletta-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Later, he taught school and studied law in <a href="/wiki/Salisbury,_North_Carolina" title="Salisbury, North Carolina">Salisbury, North Carolina</a>. In 1787, he was admitted to the bar, and moved to <a href="/wiki/Jonesborough,_Tennessee" title="Jonesborough, Tennessee">Jonesborough</a>, in what was then the <a href="/wiki/Washington_District,_North_Carolina" title="Washington District, North Carolina">Western District</a> of North Carolina. This area later became the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Territory" title="Southwest Territory">Southwest Territory</a> (1790), the precursor to the state of Tennessee.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_War_service">Revolutionary War service</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/220px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/330px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png/440px-Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1198" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew-Jackson-disobeys-British-officer-1780.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Young Jackson Refusing to Clean Major Coffin's Boots</i> (1876 <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithograph</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a>, Jackson, at age thirteen, informally helped the local militia as a courier.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> His eldest brother, Hugh, died from heat exhaustion during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stono_Ferry" title="Battle of Stono Ferry">Battle of Stono Ferry</a>, on June 20, 1779. Jackson and his brother Robert were captured by the British and held as prisoners; they nearly starved to death in captivity. When Jackson refused to clean the boots of a British officer, the officer slashed at the youth with a sword, leaving Jackson with scars on his left hand and head, as well as an intense hatred for the British.<sup id="cite_ref-Remini_1:21_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Remini_1:21-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> While imprisoned, the brothers contracted <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.</p>
<p>Robert Jackson died on April 27, 1781, a few days after their mother Elizabeth secured the brothers' release. After being assured Andrew would recover, Elizabeth Jackson volunteered to nurse <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war" class="mw-redirect">prisoners of war</a> on board two ships in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a> harbor, where there had been an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>. She died from the disease in November 1781, and was buried in an unmarked grave. Jackson became an orphan at age 14.<sup id="cite_ref-Remini_1:13_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Remini_1:13-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Following the deaths of his brothers and mother during the war, Jackson blamed the British for his losses.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_political_career">Legal and political career</span></h2>
<p>Jackson began his legal career in Jonesborough, now northeastern Tennessee. Though his <a href="/wiki/Legal_education" title="Legal education">legal education</a> was scanty, he knew enough to be a <a href="/wiki/Country_lawyer" title="Country lawyer">country lawyer</a> on the <a href="/wiki/American_frontier" title="American frontier">frontier</a>. Since he was not from a distinguished family, he had to make his career by his own merits; soon he began to prosper in the rough-and-tumble world of frontier law. Most of the actions grew out of disputed land-claims, or from <a href="/wiki/Assault_(tort)" title="Assault (tort)">assault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battery_(tort)" title="Battery (tort)">battery</a>. In 1788, he was appointed Solicitor (prosecutor) of the Western District and held the same position in the government of the <a href="/wiki/Territory_South_of_the_River_Ohio" title="Territory South of the River Ohio" class="mw-redirect">Territory South of the River Ohio</a> after 1791.</p>
<p>Jackson was elected as a delegate to the Tennessee <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_meeting)" title="Constitutional convention (political meeting)">constitutional convention</a> in 1796. When Tennessee achieved statehood that year, Jackson was elected its <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a>. The following year, he was elected <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican</a>, but he resigned within a year. (His return to the U.S. Senate in 1823, after 24 years, 11 months, 3 days out of office, marks the second longest gap in service to the chamber in history.)<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1798, he was appointed a judge of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Supreme_Court" title="Tennessee Supreme Court">Tennessee Supreme Court</a>, serving until 1804.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_speculation_and_founding_of_Memphis">Land speculation and founding of Memphis</span></h3>
<p>In 1794, Jackson formed a business with <a href="/wiki/John_Overton_(judge)" title="John Overton (judge)">John Overton</a> "for the purpose of purchasing lands as well those lands without as within military bounds"—overtly buying and selling land which had been reserved by treaty for the Cherokee and Chickasaw.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon his return from <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Florida</a>, Jackson negotiated the sale of the land from the <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw_Nation" title="Chickasaw Nation">Chickasaw Nation</a> in 1818 (termed the <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Purchase_(U.S._historical_region)" title="Jackson Purchase (U.S. historical region)">Jackson Purchase</a>). He was one of the three original investors who founded <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a> in 1819 (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_Memphis,_Tennessee" title="History of Memphis, Tennessee">History of Memphis, Tennessee</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hermitage_plantation">Hermitage plantation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/220px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" width="220" height="379" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/330px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png/440px-AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="783" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AndrewJackson-RewardNotice-EscapedSlave-1804.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Escaped slave notice placed by Andrew Jackson offering a $50 reward, plus expenses, for the return of an enslaved mulatto man who escaped from Jackson's plantation. In a move unusual for the time, the notice offers "ten dollars extra, for every hundred lashes any person will give him, to the amount of three hundred."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to his legal and political career, Jackson prospered as <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">planter</a>, slave owner, and merchant. He built a home and the first general store in <a href="/wiki/Gallatin,_Tennessee" title="Gallatin, Tennessee">Gallatin, Tennessee</a> in 1803. The next year he acquired <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">the Hermitage</a>, a 640-acre (259&#160;ha) plantation in <a href="/wiki/Davidson_County,_Tennessee" title="Davidson County, Tennessee">Davidson County</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>. Jackson later added 360 acres (146&#160;ha) to the plantation, which eventually grew to 1,050 acres (425&#160;ha). The primary crop was <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, grown by enslaved workers. Starting with nine slaves, Jackson held as many as 44 by 1820, and later held up to 150 slaves, making him among the planter elite. Throughout his lifetime Jackson may have owned as many as 300 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> men, women, and children were enslaved by Jackson on three sections of the Hermitage plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Slaves lived in extended family units between five and ten persons quartered in 20-foot-square cabins made either of brick or logs. The size and quality of Jackson's Hermitage slave quarters exceeded the standards of his times. To help slaves acquire food staples, in addition to Jackson's rations, Jackson supplied slaves with guns, knives, and fishing equipment for hunting and fishing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> At times Jackson paid his slaves with monies and coins to trade in local markets. The Hermitage plantation was a profit-making enterprise and Jackson, who demanded slave loyalty, permitted slaves to be whipped to increase productivity or if he believed his slaves' offenses were severe enough. Jackson at various times posted advertisements for his fugitive slaves. For the standards of his times Jackson was considered a humane slave owner who furnished his slaves food, housing, and did not prohibit his female slaves from childbirthing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hermitage_Slavery_2011_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hermitage_Slavery_2011-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_career">Military career</span></h2>
<p>Jackson was appointed commander of the Tennessee militia in 1801, with the rank of <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">colonel</a>. He was later elected major general of the Tennessee militia in 1802.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_1812">War of 1812</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Creek_campaign_and_treaty">Creek campaign and treaty</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg/220px-Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg/330px-Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg/440px-Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg 2x" data-file-width="967" data-file-height="826" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Creek_War_Treaty_1814.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Treaty of Fort Jackson, 1814<br />
Jackson imposed severe terms on the Creek Indians <i>Treaty with the Creeks</i> (1847)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">chief</a> <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a> encouraged the "<a href="/wiki/Red_Stick" title="Red Stick" class="mw-redirect">Red Stick</a>" Creek Indians of northern Alabama and Georgia to attack white settlements. He had unified tribes in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territory" title="Northwest Territory">Northwest</a> to rise up against the Americans, trying to repel American settlers from those lands north of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio</a>. Four hundred settlers were killed in the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Mims_massacre" title="Fort Mims massacre">Fort Mims massacre</a>—one of the few instances of Native Americans killing a large number of American settlers and their African-American slaves<sup id="cite_ref-Indian_massacres:_1500.E2.80.931830_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indian_massacres:_1500.E2.80.931830-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup>—which brought the United States into the internal <a href="/wiki/Creek_War" title="Creek War">Creek campaign</a>. Occurring at the same time as the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, the Creek campaign saw Jackson command the U.S. forces, which included the Tennessee militia, U.S. <a href="/wiki/Regular_Army_(United_States)" title="Regular Army (United States)">regulars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Creek" title="Lower Creek" class="mw-redirect">Lower Creek</a> warriors. <a href="/wiki/Sam_Houston" title="Sam Houston">Sam Houston</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Crockett" title="David Crockett" class="mw-redirect">David Crockett</a> served under Jackson in this campaign.</p>
<p>Jackson defeated the Red Sticks at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Horseshoe_Bend_(1814)" title="Battle of Horseshoe Bend (1814)">Battle of Horseshoe Bend</a> in 1814. US forces and their allies killed 800 Red Stick warriors in this battle, but spared the chief <a href="/wiki/William_Weatherford" title="William Weatherford">Red Eagle</a>, a mixed-race man also known as <a href="/wiki/William_Weatherford" title="William Weatherford">William Weatherford</a>. After the victory, Madison's Secretary of War Armstrong ordered Major General Thomas Pinckney in April 1814 to make the surrender treaty. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Pinckney made moderate terms of surrender including handing over an unspecified amount of land, the construction of U.S. forts, turning over warriors who instigated hostilities, and agreeing to stop trade with foreign countries. Jackson opposed the unpopular Pinckney treaty, desiring to completely destroy the Creek nation. Jackson was promoted Major General and given charge of the Seventh Military District, replacing Major General Thomas Flournoy. Jackson, now commanding general, immediately threw out Pinckney's treaty and forced severe terms upon both the Upper Creek enemies and the Lower Creek allies, wresting twenty-two million acres in present-day Georgia and Alabama from all the Creek for European-American settlement. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson also confiscated land from Indians who had sided with the Americans. Jackson stated that the terms must be accepted or the tribe would be forcefully removed to Florida. On August 9, 1814, 35 Indian elder leaders signed Jackson's <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fort_Jackson" title="Treaty of Fort Jackson">Treaty of Fort Jackson</a>. The warrior faction of the Creek nation and the British, however, did not formally recognize the treaty. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDSHeidler_JTHeidler_1997192-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to author <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Jahoda" title="Gloria Jahoda">Gloria Jahoda</a>, the Creeks coined their own name for him, <i>Jacksa Chula Harjo</i> or "Jackson, old and fierce".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_New_Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/220px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/330px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg/440px-Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3488" data-file-height="2579" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_New_Orleans.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a></i>. General Andrew Jackson stands on the parapet of his makeshift defenses as his troops repulse attacking <a href="/wiki/93rd_(Sutherland_Highlanders)_Regiment_of_Foot" title="93rd (Sutherland Highlanders) Regiment of Foot">Highlanders</a>, by painter <a href="/wiki/Edward_Percy_Moran" title="Edward Percy Moran">Edward Percy Moran</a> in 1910.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson's service in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> against the United Kingdom was conspicuous for bravery and success. When British forces threatened <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, Jackson took command of the defenses, including militia from several western states and territories. He was a strict officer but was popular with his troops. They said he was "tough as old hickory" wood on the battlefield, and he acquired the nickname of "Old Hickory". In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">Battle of New Orleans</a> on January 8, 1815, Jackson's 5,000 soldiers won a decisive victory over 7,500 British. At the end of the battle, the British had 2,037 casualties: 291 dead (including three senior generals), 1,262 wounded, and 484 captured or missing. The Americans had 71 casualties: 13 dead, 39 wounded, and 19 missing.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforced_martial_law_New_Orleans">Enforced martial law New Orleans</span></h4>
<p>Jackson ordered the arrest of U. S. District Court Judge <a href="/wiki/Dominic_Augustin_Hall" title="Dominic Augustin Hall">Dominic A. Hall</a> in March 1815, after the judge signed a writ of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a> on behalf of a Louisiana legislator that Jackson had arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Louis Louaillier had written an anonymous piece in the New Orleans newspaper, challenging Jackson's refusal to release the militia, after the British ceded the field of battle.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson had claimed the authority to declare <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a> over the entire City of New Orleans, not merely his "camp."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> After ordering the arrest of a Louisiana legislator, a federal judge, a lawyer and after intervention of <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Lewis_(judge)" title="Joshua Lewis (judge)">Joshua Lewis</a>, a State Judge, who was simultaneously serving under Jackson in the militia, and who also signed a writ of habeas corpus against Jackson, his commanding officer, seeking Judge Hall's release, Jackson relented.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Civilian authorities in New Orleans had reason to fear Jackson. But they fared better than did the six members of the militia whose executions, ordered by Jackson, would surface as the <a href="/wiki/Coffin_Handbills" title="Coffin Handbills">Coffin Handbills</a> during his 1828 Presidential campaign. Nonetheless, Jackson became a national hero for his actions in this battle and the War of 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> By a resolution on February 27, 1815, Jackson received the <a href="/wiki/Thanks_of_Congress" title="Thanks of Congress">Thanks of Congress</a> as well as a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a>, "underwhelmed" by Jackson, later commented in <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_in_America" title="Democracy in America">Democracy in America</a></i> that Jackson "...&#160;was raised to the Presidency, and has been maintained there, solely by the recollection of a victory which he gained, twenty years ago, under the walls of New Orleans."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Seminole_War">First Seminole War</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambristertrial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/220px-Ambristertrial.jpg" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/330px-Ambristertrial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Ambristertrial.jpg/440px-Ambristertrial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="472" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ambristertrial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trial of Robert Ambrister during the Seminole War. Ambrister was one of two British subjects executed by General Jackson. (1848)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson served in the military again during the <a href="/wiki/First_Seminole_War" title="First Seminole War" class="mw-redirect">First Seminole War</a>. He was ordered by President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> in December 1817 to lead a campaign in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> and Creek Indians. Jackson was also charged with preventing <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a> from becoming a refuge for runaway slaves. Critics later alleged that Jackson exceeded orders in his Florida actions. His directions were to "terminate the conflict".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson believed the best way to do this was to seize Florida. Before going, Jackson wrote to Monroe, "Let it be signified to me through any channel&#160;... that the possession of the Floridas would be desirable to the United States, and in sixty days it will be accomplished."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe gave Jackson orders that were purposely ambiguous, sufficient for international denials.</p>
<p>The Seminole attacked Jackson's Tennessee volunteers. The Seminole attack left their villages vulnerable, and Jackson burned their houses and the crops. He found letters that indicated that the Spanish and British were secretly assisting the Indians. Jackson believed that the United States could not be secure as long as <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">British</a> encouraged Indians to fight, and argued that his actions were undertaken in self-defense. Jackson captured <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">Pensacola, Florida</a>, with little more than some warning shots, and deposed the Spanish governor. He captured and then tried and executed two British subjects, <a href="/wiki/Arbuthnot_and_Ambrister_incident" title="Arbuthnot and Ambrister incident">Robert Ambrister and Alexander Arbuthnot</a>, who had been supplying and advising the Indians. Jackson's actions struck fear into the Seminole tribes as word spread of his ruthlessness in battle (he became known as "Sharp Knife").</p>
<p>The executions, and Jackson's invasion of territory belonging to Spain, a country with which the U.S. was not at war, created an international incident. Many in the Monroe administration called for Jackson to be <a href="/wiki/Censure_in_the_United_States" title="Censure in the United States">censured</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, an early believer in <a href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny" class="mw-redirect">Manifest Destiny</a>, defended Jackson. When the Spanish minister demanded a "suitable punishment" for Jackson, Adams wrote back, "Spain must immediately [decide] either to place a force in Florida adequate at once to the protection of her territory&#160;... or cede to the United States a province, of which she retains nothing but the nominal possession, but which is, in fact&#160;... a post of annoyance to them."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams used Jackson's conquest, and Spain's own weakness, to get Spain to cede Florida to the United States by the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>. Jackson was subsequently named Florida's military governor and served from March 10, 1821, to December 31, 1821.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1824">Election of 1824</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">United States presidential election, 1824</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/170px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/255px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Andrew_Jackson.jpg/340px-Andrew_Jackson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="739" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson in 1824, painting by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sully" title="Thomas Sully">Thomas Sully</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_legislature" title="Tennessee legislature" class="mw-redirect">Tennessee legislature</a> nominated Jackson for President in 1822. It also elected him U.S. Senator again. By 1824, the Democratic-Republican Party had become the only functioning national party. Its Presidential candidates had been chosen by an informal <a href="/wiki/Congressional_nominating_caucus" title="Congressional nominating caucus">Congressional nominating caucus</a>, but this had become unpopular. In 1824, most of the Democratic-Republicans in Congress boycotted the caucus. Those who attended backed Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a> for President and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> for Vice President. A Pennsylvania convention nominated Jackson for President a month later, stating that the irregular caucus ignored the "voice of the people" and was a "vain hope that the American people might be thus deceived into a belief that he [Crawford] was the regular democratic candidate".<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Gallatin criticized Jackson as "an honest man and the idol of the worshipers of military glory, but from incapacity, military habits, and habitual disregard of laws and constitutional provisions, altogether unfit for the office".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Besides Jackson and Crawford, the Secretary of State John Quincy Adams and House Speaker <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> were also candidates. Jackson received the most popular votes (but not a majority, and four states had no popular ballot). The <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electoral votes</a> were split four ways, with Jackson having a plurality. Because no candidate received a majority, the election was decided by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, which chose Adams. Jackson supporters denounced this result as a "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_bargain" title="Corrupt bargain" class="mw-redirect">corrupt bargain</a>" because Clay gave his state's support to Adams, who subsequently appointed Clay as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State" title="Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>. As none of Kentucky's electors had initially voted for Adams, and Jackson had won the popular vote, some Kentucky politicians criticized Clay for violating the will of the people in return for personal political favors. Jackson's defeat burnished his political credentials, however; many voters believed the "man of the people" had been robbed by the "corrupt aristocrats of the East".</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1828">Election of 1828</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">United States presidential election, 1828</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_presidential_campaign,_1828" title="Andrew Jackson presidential campaign, 1828">Andrew Jackson presidential campaign, 1828</a></div>
<p>Jackson denounced the "corrupt bargain" that put Adams in the White House and laid plans for a crusade to oust Adams from office.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> After resigning the Senate in October 1825, he continued his quest for the Presidency. The Tennessee legislature again nominated Jackson for President. He attracted Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Ritchie" title="Thomas Ritchie">Thomas Ritchie</a> into his camp (Van Buren and Ritchie were previous supporters of Crawford). Van Buren, with help from his friends in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, revived the old Republican Party, gave it a new name as the Democratic Party, "restored party rivalries", and forged a national organization of durability.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The Jackson coalition handily defeated Adams in 1828.</p>
<p>During the election, Jackson's opponents referred to him as a "<a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">jackass</a>". Jackson liked the name and used the jackass as a symbol for a while, but it died out. However, it later became the symbol for the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> when cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nast" title="Thomas Nast">Thomas Nast</a> popularized it.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The campaign was very much a personal one. As was the custom at the time, neither candidate personally campaigned, but their political followers organized many campaign events. Both candidates were rhetorically attacked in the press, which reached a low point when the press accused Jackson's wife Rachel of bigamy. Though the accusation was true, as were most personal attacks leveled against him during the campaign, it was based on events that occurred many years prior (1791 to 1794). Jackson said he would forgive those who insulted him, but he would never forgive the ones who attacked his wife. Rachel died suddenly on December 22, 1828, before his inauguration, and was buried on Christmas Eve. He blamed the Adams campaigners for her death. "May God Almighty forgive her murderers," he swore at her funeral. "I never can."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson also came under heavy attack as a slave trader who bought and sold slaves and moved them about in defiance of modern standards or morality. (He was not attacked for merely owning slaves used in plantation work.)<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1829.E2.80.931837">Presidency 1829–1837</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Andrew_Jackson" title="Presidency of Andrew Jackson">Presidency of Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg/220px-Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg/330px-Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="417" data-file-height="547" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Jackson_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Andrew Jackson<br />
New York: Ritchie &amp; Co. (1860)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson's name has been associated with <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a> or the spread of democracy in terms of the passing of political power from established elites to ordinary voters based in political parties. "The Age of Jackson" shaped the national agenda and American politics. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002101_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002101-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's philosophy as President followed much in the same line as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, advocating Republican values held by the Revolutionary War generation. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's presidency held a high moralistic tone; having as a planter himself agrarian sympathies, a limited view of states rights and the federal government. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson feared that monied and business interests would corrupt <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republican values</a>. When South Carolina opposed the tariff law he took a strong line in favor of nationalism and against secession.</p>
<p>Jackson believed that the president's authority was derived from the people and the presidential office was above party politics. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Instead of choosing party favorites, Jackson chose "plain, businessmen" whom he intended to control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002104-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson chose <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/John_Eaton_(politician)" title="John Eaton (politician)">John Eaton</a> Secretary of War, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Ingham" title="Samuel Ingham">Samuel Ingham</a> Secretary of Treasury, <a href="/wiki/John_Branch" title="John Branch">John Branch</a> Secretary of Navy, <a href="/wiki/John_MacPherson_Berrien" title="John MacPherson Berrien" class="mw-redirect">John Berrien</a> as Attorney General, and <a href="/wiki/William_Taylor_Barry" title="William Taylor Barry" class="mw-redirect">William T. Barry</a> as postmaster general. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's first choice of Cabinet proved to be unsuccessful, full of bitter partisanship and gossip, especially between Eaton, Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, and Van Buren. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> By the Spring of 1831, only Barry remained, while the rest of Jackson's cabinet had been discharged.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105.2C_108_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105.2C_108-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's following cabinet selections worked better together.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002105-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration">Inauguration</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Andrew_Jackson" title="First inauguration of Andrew Jackson">First inauguration of Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<p>On March 4, 1829, Andrew Jackson became the first United States president-elect to take the oath of office on the East Portico of the U.S. Capitol.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson was the first President to invite the public to attend the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> ball honoring his first inauguration. Many poor people came to the inaugural ball in their homemade clothes. The crowd became so large that Jackson's guards could not keep them out of the White House, which became so crowded with people that dishes and decorative pieces inside were eventually broken. Some people stood on good chairs in muddied boots just to get a look at the President. The crowd had become so wild that the attendants poured punch in tubs and put it on the White House lawn to lure people outside. Jackson's raucous populism earned him the nickname "King Mob".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petticoat_politics">Petticoat politics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Eaton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/John_Eaton.jpg/220px-John_Eaton.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/John_Eaton.jpg/330px-John_Eaton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/John_Eaton.jpg/440px-John_Eaton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="871" data-file-height="1157" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Eaton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Secretary of War<br />
<a href="/wiki/John_H._Eaton" title="John H. Eaton" class="mw-redirect">John H. Eaton</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Petticoat affair</a></div>
<p>Jackson devoted a considerable amount of his presidential time during his first years in office responding to what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Eaton affair</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Vicious Washington gossip circulated among Jackson's Cabinet members and their wives including Vice President <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>'s wife <a href="/wiki/Floride_Calhoun" title="Floride Calhoun">Floride</a> concerning Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/John_H._Eaton" title="John H. Eaton" class="mw-redirect">John H. Eaton</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Margaret_O%27Neill_Eaton" title="Margaret O'Neill Eaton">Peggy</a>. Bitter rumors spread that Peggy was loose in her morals while working at her father's tavern when her naval officer husband was away at sea. After Peggy's husband died at sea, both Eaton and Peggy were criticized for their perceived early marriage. Initially Jackson thought that <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> was behind the gossip, but then he eventually concluded Calhoun was responsible for spreading the rumors. Both Jackson and Van Buren defended Eaton and his wife Peggy, however Calhoun's wife Floride and the wives of Jackson's pro-Calhoun Cabinet members publicly shunned both Eaton and Peggy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002107-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren, Eaton, and Calhoun resigned office over the affair while Jackson dismissed the rest of his pro-Calhoun cabinet members except Postmaster <a href="/wiki/William_T._Barry" title="William T. Barry">William T. Barry</a>. Jackson nominated Van Buren to be Minister to England, however, Calhoun's supporters in the Senate blocked the nomination giving Calhoun, who had continued to be Vice President and President of the Senate until December 1832, the deciding vote against Van Buren and blocked the nomination. Calhoun publically castigated and boasted that Van Buren's political career was over, stating the defeated nomination would "...kill him, sir, kill dead. He will never kick, sir, never kick." <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> This rumor however proved to be false as Van Buren, who played a leading role in the Jackson's unofficial <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_Cabinet" title="Kitchen Cabinet">Kitchen Cabinet</a>, allied with Jackson and became Jackson's successor and Vice Presidential candidate in 1832. Jackson also acquired the <i>Globe</i> paper that was sympathetic with him.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_removal_policy">Indian removal policy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">Indian removal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trails_of_Tears_en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/220px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/330px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Trails_of_Tears_en.png/440px-Trails_of_Tears_en.png 2x" data-file-width="2025" data-file-height="1547" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trails_of_Tears_en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson's Indian Removal Act and subsequent treaties resulted in the transplantation of several <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Indian tribes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout his eight years in office, Jackson made about 70 treaties with native American tribes both in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Southwest" title="Old Southwest">South</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Northwest" title="Old Northwest" class="mw-redirect">Northwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's presidency marked a new era in Indian-<a href="/wiki/European_American" title="European American">Anglo</a> American relations initiating a policy of Indian removal. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson himself sometimes participated in the treaty negotiating process with various Indian tribes, though other times he left the negotiations to his subordinates. The southern tribes included the <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>. The northwest tribes include the <a href="/wiki/Chippewa" title="Chippewa" class="mw-redirect">Chippewa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Potawatomi" title="Potawatomi">Potawatomi</a>. Though conflict between Indians and American settlers took place in the north and in the south, the problem was worse in the south where the Indian populations were larger. Indian wars broke out repeatedly, often when native tribes, especially the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muscogee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> Indians, refused to abide by the treaties for various reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Seminole_War" title="Second Seminole War">Second Seminole War</a>, started in December 1835, lasted over six years finally ending in August 1842 under President <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002110-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Though relations between Europeans (and later Americans) and Indians were always complicated, they grew increasingly complicated once American settlements began pushing further west in the years after the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. Often these relations were peaceful, though they increasingly grew tense and sometimes even violent, both on the part of American settlers and the Indians. From <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> to John Quincy Adams, the problem was typically ignored or dealt with lightly; though by Jackson's time the earlier policy had grown unsustainable. The problem was especially acute in the south (in particular the lands near the state of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>), where Indian populations were larger, denser, and more Americanized than those of the north. As such, there had developed a growing popular and political movement to deal with the problem, and out of this developed a policy to relocate certain Indian populations. Jackson, never one known for timidity, became an advocate for this relocation policy in what is considered by some historians to be the most controversial aspect of his presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> This contrasted from his immediate predecessor, President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Q. Adams</a>, who tended to follow the policy of his own predecessors, that of letting the problem play itself out with minimal intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's presidency thus took place in a new era in Indian-<a href="/wiki/European_American" title="European American">Anglo</a> American relations, in that it marked federal action and a policy of relocation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108_47-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002108-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> As such, during Jackson's presidency, Indian relations between the Southern tribes and the state governments had reached a critical juncture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his December 8, 1829, First Annual Message to Congress, Jackson advocated land west of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> be set aside for Indian tribes. Congress had been developing its own Indian relocation bill, and Jackson had many supporters in both the Senate and House who agreed with his goal. On May 26, 1830 Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>, and Jackson signed it into law. The Act authorized the President to negotiate treaties to buy tribal lands in the east in exchange for lands further west, outside of existing U.S. state borders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The passage of the bill was Jackson's first successful legislative triumph and marked the Democratic party's emergence into American political society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002109-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> The passage of the act was especially popular in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a> where population growth and the discovery of gold on <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> land had increased pressure on tribal lands.</p>
<p>The state of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> became involved in a contentious jurisdictional dispute with the Cherokees, culminating in the 1832 <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Supreme Court</a> decision (<i><a href="/wiki/Worcester_v._Georgia" title="Worcester v. Georgia">Worcester v. Georgia</a></i>). In that decision, <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Supreme Court</a> <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, in writing for the court, ruled that Georgia could not impose its laws upon Cherokee tribal lands.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson is frequently, though incorrectly, attributed with the following response: "John Marshall has made his decision, now let him enforce it". The quote originated in 1863 from <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>. Jackson used the Georgia crisis to broker an agreement whereby the Cherokee leaders agreed to a removal treaty. A group of Cherokees led by <a href="/wiki/John_Ridge" title="John Ridge">John Ridge</a> negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_New_Echota" title="Treaty of New Echota">Treaty of New Echota</a> with Jackson's representatives. Ridge was not a widely recognized leader of the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_Nation_(19th_century)" title="Cherokee Nation (19th century)" class="mw-redirect">Cherokee Nation</a>, and this document was rejected by some as illegitimate.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> A group of Cherokees petitioned in protest of the proposed removal, though this wasn't taken up by the Supreme Court or the U.S. Congress, in part due to delays and timing.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS-JDIR_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-JDIR-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The treaty was enforced by Jackson's successor, Van Buren, who sent 7,000 troops to carry out the relocation policy. Due to the infighting between political factions, many Cherokees thought their appeals were still being considered when the relocation began.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> It was subsequent to this that as many as 4,000 Cherokees died on the "<a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>". By the 1830s, under constant pressure from settlers, each of the five southern tribes had ceded most of its lands, but sizable self-government groups lived in Georgia, Alabama, Mississippi, and Florida. All of these (except the Seminoles) had moved far in the coexistence with whites, and they resisted suggestions that they should voluntarily remove themselves. Their methods earned them the title of the "<a href="/wiki/Five_Civilized_Tribes" title="Five Civilized Tribes">Five Civilized Tribes</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-PBS-JDIR_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-JDIR-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> More than 45,000 American Indians were relocated to the West during Jackson's administration, though a few Cherokees walked back afterwards or migrated to the high <a href="/wiki/Great_Smoky_Mountains" title="Great Smoky Mountains">Smoky Mountains</a> along the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> border.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's initiatives to deal with the conflicts between Indians and American settlers has been a source of controversy<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> on and off over the years,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> especially among his political opponents at the time and ideological opponents since.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Modern historians,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schlesinger,_Jr." title="Arthur Schlesinger, Jr." class="mw-redirect">Arthur Schlesinger, Jr.</a>, often note the history of American conflicts with Indians<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> dating to long before the American revolution, and the ultimate need for a solution which Jackson and congress partly achieved. Starting around 1970, the controversy picked up again, this time with more ideological tones. Around that time, Jackson came under sharp attack from revisionist writers on the left, such as Michael Paul Rogin and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Zinn" title="Howard Zinn">Howard Zinn</a>, often on on this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1969 <a href="/wiki/Francis_Paul_Prucha" title="Francis Paul Prucha">Francis Paul Prucha</a> argued that Jackson's removal of the "Five Civilized Tribes" from the very hostile white environment in the Old South to Oklahoma probably saved their very existence.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
