<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiated_reforms">Initiated reforms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 250px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -75px; left: -45px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:JACKSON,_Andrew-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Jackson as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="386" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-JACKSON%2C_Andrew-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7636" data-file-height="9066" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JACKSON,_Andrew-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:JACKSON, Andrew-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:JACKSON, Andrew-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Jackson as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In an effort to purge the government from corruption of previous administrations, Jackson launched presidential investigations into all executive Cabinet offices and departments. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465.E2.80.9366_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465.E2.80.9366-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> During Jackson's tenure in office, large amounts of public money were put in the hands of public officials. Jackson, who believed appointees should be hired by merit, withdrew many candidates he believed were lax in their handling of monies. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465.E2.80.9366_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465.E2.80.9366-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson asked Congress to reform embezzlement laws, reduce fraudulent applications for federal pensions, revenue laws to prevent evasion of custom duties, and laws to improve government accounting. Jackson's Postmaster Barry resigned after a Congressional investigation into the postal service revealed mismanagement of mail services, collusion and favoritism in awarding lucrative contracts, failure to audit accounts and supervise contract performances. Jackson replaced Barry with <a href="/wiki/Amos_Kendall" title="Amos Kendall">Amos Kendall</a>, who went on to implement much needed reforms in the Postal Service. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197467_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197467-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson repeatedly called for the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">Electoral College</a> by constitutional amendment in his annual messages to Congress as President.<sup id="cite_ref-firstaddress_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstaddress-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> In his third annual message to Congress, he expressed the view "I have heretofore recommended amendments of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Federal Constitution</a> giving the election of President and Vice-President to the people and limiting the service of the former to a single term. So important do I consider these changes in our fundamental law that I can not, in accordance with my sense of duty, omit to press them upon the consideration of a new Congress."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's time in the presidency as saw various improvements in financial provisions for veterans and their dependents. The Service Pension Act of 1832, for instance, provided pensions to veterans "even where there existed no obvious financial or physical need,"<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> while an Act of July 1836 enabled widows of Revolutionary War soldiers who met certain criteria to receive their husband's pensions.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation_in_office_and_spoils_system">Rotation in office and spoils system</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">Spoils system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rotation_in_office" title="Rotation in office" class="mw-redirect">Rotation in office</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1834,_Frazee,_John,_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/220px-1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/330px-1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/440px-1834%2C_Frazee%2C_John%2C_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1426" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1834,_Frazee,_John,_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
bust by <a href="/wiki/John_Frazee" title="John Frazee">John Frazee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University_Art_Museum" title="Princeton University Art Museum">Princeton University Art Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon assuming the presidency in 1829 Jackson enforced the <a href="/wiki/Tenure_of_Office_Act_(1820)" title="Tenure of Office Act (1820)"><i>Tenure of Office Act</i></a>, passed earlier into law by President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> in 1820, that limited appointed office tenure and authorized the president to remove and appoint political party associates. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson believed that a <a href="/wiki/Rotation_in_office" title="Rotation in office" class="mw-redirect">rotation in office</a> was actually a democratic reform preventing father-to-son succession of office and made civil service responsible to the popular will. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson declared that rotation of appointments in political office was "a leading principle in the republican creed".<sup id="cite_ref-firstaddress_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstaddress-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson noted, "In a country where offices are created solely for the benefit of the people no one man has any more intrinsic right to official station than another."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson believed that rotating political appointments would prevent the development of a corrupt bureaucracy. Opposed to this view however, were Jackson's supporters who in order to strengthen party loyalty wanted to give the posts to other party members. In practice, this would have meant the continuation of the patronage system by replacing federal employees with friends or party loyalists.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> The number of federal office holders removed by Jackson were exaggerated by his opponents; Jackson only rotated about 20% of federal office holders during his first term, some for dereliction of duty rather than political purposes. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461.E2.80.9362_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461.E2.80.9362-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson, however, did use his image and presidential power to award his loyal Democratic Party followers by granting them federal office appointments. Jackson's democratic approach incorporated patriotism for country as qualification for holding office. Having appointed a soldier who had lost his leg fighting on the battlefield to a postmastership Jackson stated "If he lost his leg fighting for his country, that is ... enough for me." <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson's theory regarding rotation of office generated what would later be called the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>, a practice that Jackson, ironically, didn't justify. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197461-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The political realities of Washington, however, ultimately forced Jackson to make partisan appointments despite his personal reservations.<sup id="cite_ref-howe2007_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howe2007-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Historians believe Jackson's presidency marked the beginning of an era of decline in public ethics. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Supervision of bureaus and departments whose operations were outside of Washington (such as the New York Customs House; the Postal Service; the Departments of Navy and War; and the Bureau of Indian Affairs, whose budget had enormously increased in the past two decades) proved to be difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197465-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Other aspects of the spoils system including the buying of offices, forced political party campaign participation, and collection of assessments, did not take place until after Jackson's presidency. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197462_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197462-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> During Jackson's presidency, those in opposition to Jackson's purging of office holders, formed the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a>, calling Jackson "King Andrew I" having feared his military background, and named their party after the English parliamentary Whigs who opposed eighteenth century British monarchy. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197462_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197462-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nullification_crisis">Nullification crisis</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a></div>
<p>Another notable crisis during Jackson's period of office was the "<a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a>", or "secession crisis", of 1828–1832, which merged issues of sectional strife with disagreements over tariffs. Critics alleged that high tariffs (the "<a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_Abominations" title="Tariff of Abominations">Tariff of Abominations</a>") on imports of common manufactured goods made in Europe made those goods more expensive than ones from the northern U.S., raising the prices paid by planters in the South. Southern politicians argued that tariffs benefited northern industrialists at the expense of southern farmers.</p>
<p>The issue came to a head when Vice President Calhoun, in the <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_Exposition_and_Protest" title="South Carolina Exposition and Protest">South Carolina Exposition and Protest</a> of 1828, supported the claim of his home state, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, that it had the right to "nullify"—declare void—the tariff legislation of 1828, and more generally the right of a state to nullify any Federal laws that went against its interests. Although Jackson sympathized with the South in the tariff debate, he also vigorously supported a strong union, with effective powers for the central government. Jackson attempted to face down Calhoun over the issue, which developed into a bitter rivalry between the two men.</p>
<p>Particularly notable was an incident at the April 13, 1830, Jefferson Day dinner, involving after-dinner toasts. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hayne" title="Robert Hayne" class="mw-redirect">Robert Hayne</a> began by toasting to "The Union of the States, and the Sovereignty of the States". Jackson then rose, and in a booming voice added "Our federal Union: It must be preserved!"&#160;– a clear challenge to Calhoun. Calhoun clarified his position by responding "The Union: Next to our Liberty, the most dear!"<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/1832_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1832 Democratic National Convention">first Democratic National Convention</a>, which was privately engineered by members of the Kitchen Cabinet, Calhoun and Jackson broke from each other politically and Van Buren replaced Calhoun as Jackson's running mate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">1832 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> In December 1832, Calhoun resigned as Vice President to become a U.S. Senator for South Carolina.</p>
<p>In response to South Carolina's nullification claim, Jackson vowed to send troops to <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> to enforce the laws. In December 1832, he issued a resounding proclamation against the "nullifiers", stating that he considered "the power to annul a law of the United States, assumed by one State, incompatible with the existence of the Union, contradicted expressly by the letter of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a>, unauthorized by its spirit, inconsistent with every principle on which it was founded, and destructive of the great object for which it was formed". South Carolina, the President declared, stood on "the brink of insurrection and treason", and he appealed to the people of the state to reassert their allegiance to that Union for which their ancestors had fought. Jackson also denied the right of secession: "The Constitution&#160;... forms a <i>government</i> not a league&#160;... To say that any State may at pleasure secede from the Union is to say that the United States is not a nation."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson asked Congress to pass a "<a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">Force Bill</a>" explicitly authorizing the use of military force to enforce the tariff, but its passage was delayed until <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionists</a> led by Clay agreed to a reduced <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1833" title="Tariff of 1833">Compromise Tariff</a>. The Force Bill and Compromise Tariff passed on March 1, 1833, and Jackson signed both. The South Carolina Convention then met and rescinded its nullification ordinance. The Force Bill became moot because it was no longer needed. On May 1, 1833, Jackson wrote, "the tariff was only the pretext, and <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">disunion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">southern confederacy</a> the real object. The next pretext will be the negro, or <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_the_United_States" title="History of slavery in the United States" class="mw-redirect">slavery</a> question."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamCRives.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/220px-WilliamCRives.png" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/330px-WilliamCRives.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/WilliamCRives.png/440px-WilliamCRives.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="839" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WilliamCRives.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jackson's Minister to France <a href="/wiki/William_Cabell_Rives" title="William Cabell Rives">William C. Rives</a> successfully negotiated a reparations treaty with France in 1831.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Jackson took office in 1829 spoliation claims, or compensation demands for the capture of American ships and sailors, dating from the Napoleonic era, caused strained relations between the U.S. and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> governments. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The French Navy had captured and sent American ships to Spanish ports while holding their crews captive forcing them to labor without any charges or judicial rules. According to Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> relations between the U.S. and France were "hopeless".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's Minister to France <a href="/wiki/William_Cabell_Rives" title="William Cabell Rives">William C. Rives</a>, however, through diplomacy was able to convince the French government to sign a reparations treaty on July 4, 1831 that would award the U.S. ? 25,000,000 ($5,000,000) in damages.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunningham_1999_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunningham_1999-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The French government became delinquent in payment due to internal financial and political difficulties. The French king <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe_I" title="Louis Philippe I">Louis Philippe I</a> and his ministers blamed the French Chamber of Deputies. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1834 the non-payment of reparations by the French government drew Jackson's ire and he became impatient. In his December 1834 State of the Union address, Jackson sternly reprimanded the French government for non-payment, stating the federal government was "wholly disappointed" by the French, and demanded Congress authorize trade reprisals against France. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Feeling insulted by Jackson's words, the French people demanded an apology. In his December 1835 State of the Union Address, Jackson refused to apologize, stating he had a good opinion of the French people and his intentions were peaceful. Jackson described in lengthy and minute detail the history of events surrounding the treaty and his belief that the French government was purposely stalling payment. The French government accepted Jackson's statements as sincere and in February 1836, American reparations were finally paid. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002119.E2.80.9320-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to France, the Jackson administration successfully settled spoliation claims with <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's state department was active and successful at making trade agreements with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siam" title="Siam" class="mw-redirect">Siam</a>. Under the treaty of Great Britain, American trade was reopened in the West Indies. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The trade agreement with Siam was America's first treaty between the United States and an Asiatic country. As a result, American exports increased 75% while imports increased 250%. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson, however, was unsuccessful in opening trade with <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson was unsuccessful at thwarting Great Britain's presence and power in South America. Jackson's attempt to purchase Texas from Mexico for $5,000,000 failed. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's agent in Texas, Colonel Anthony Butler, suggested to take Texas over militarily, but Jackson refused. Butler was later replaced toward the end of Jackson's presidency. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bank_veto_and_Election_of_1832">Bank veto and Election of 1832</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bank_War" title="Bank War">Bank War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banking_in_the_Jacksonian_Era" title="Banking in the Jacksonian Era">Banking in the Jacksonian Era</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1832bank1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/220px-1832bank1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/330px-1832bank1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/1832bank1.jpg/440px-1832bank1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1955" data-file-height="1309" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1832bank1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1833 Democratic cartoon shows Jackson destroying the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">devil's Bank</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1816 the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Second Bank of the United States</a> was charted by President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> to restore the United States economy devastated by the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002111_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002111-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1823 President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Biddle_(banker)" title="Nicholas Biddle (banker)">Nicholas Biddle</a>, the Bank's third and last executive, to run the bank. In January 1832 Biddle, on advice from his friends, submitted to Congress a renewal of the Bank's charter four years before the original 20-year charter was to end. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Biddle's recharter bill passed the Senate on June 11 and the House on July 3, 1832.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson, believing that Bank was fundamentally a corrupt monopoly whose stock was mostly held by foreigners, vetoed the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson used the issue to promote his democratic values, believing the Bank was being run exclusively for the wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson stated the Bank made "the rich richer and the potent more powerful."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican Party</a> immediately made Jackson's veto of the Bank a political issue, attempting to undermine Jackson's popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's political opponents castigated Jackson's veto as "the very slang of the leveller and demagogue," claiming Jackson was using class warfare to gain support from the common man.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1832" title="United States presidential election, 1832">1832 Presidential Election</a> the rechartering of the Second National Bank became the primary issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The election also demonstrated the rapid development and organization of political parties during this time period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The Democratic Party's first national convention, held in Baltimore, in May 1832 nominated Jackson of Tennessee and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> of New York. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The National Republican Party, who had held their first convention in Baltimore earlier in December 1831, nominated <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, senator from <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the House</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Sergeant_(politician)" title="John Sergeant (politician)">John Sergeant</a> of Pennsylvania. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112_93-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> The Anti-Mason party, who had earlier held their convention also in Baltimore in September 1831, nominated <a href="/wiki/William_Wirt_(Attorney_General)" title="William Wirt (Attorney General)">William Wirt</a> of Maryland and Amos Elmaker of Pennsylvania; both Jackson and Clay were masons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002112.E2.80.9313-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The two rival parties, however, proved to be no match for Jackson's popularity and the Democratic Party's strong political networks known as <i>Hickory Clubs</i> in state and local organization. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Democratic newspapers, parades, barbecues, and rallies increased Jackson's popularity. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson himself made numerous popular public appearances on his return trip from Tennessee to Washington D.C. Jackson won the election decisively by a landslide, receiving 55 percent of the popular vote and 219 electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Clay received 37 percent of the popular vote and 49 electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Wirt received only 8 percent of the popular vote and 7 electoral votes while the Anti-Masonic Party folded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002113-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson believed the solid victory was a popular mandate for his veto of the Bank's recharter and his continued warfare on the Bank's control over the national economy. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis_197463_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis_197463-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal_of_deposits_and_censure">Removal of deposits and censure</span></h3>
<p>In 1833, Jackson removed federal deposits from the bank, whose money-lending functions were taken over by the legions of local and state banks that materialized across America, thus drastically increasing credit and speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Three years later, Jackson issued the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">Specie Circular</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)" class="mw-redirect">executive order</a> that required buyers of government lands to pay in "specie" (gold or silver coins). The result was a great demand for specie, which many banks did not have enough of to exchange for their notes, causing the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>, which threw the national economy into a deep <a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">depression</a>. It took years for the economy to recover from the damage, however the bulk of the damage was blamed on <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>, who took office in 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Whitehouse.gov" title="Whitehouse.gov" class="mw-redirect">Whitehouse.gov</a> notes,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Basically the trouble was the 19th-century cyclical economy of "boom and bust," which was following its regular pattern, but Jackson's financial measures contributed to the crash. His destruction of the Second Bank of the United States had removed restrictions upon the inflationary practices of some state banks; wild speculation in lands, based on easy bank credit, had swept the West. To end this speculation, Jackson in 1836 had issued a Specie Circular requiring that lands be purchased with hard money—gold or silver. In 1837 the panic began. Hundreds of banks and businesses failed. Thousands lost their lands. For about five years the United States was wracked by the worst depression thus far in its history.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>—&#160;<i>Whitehouse.gov</i> official biography of <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The U.S. Senate censured Jackson on March 28, 1834, for his action in removing U.S. funds from the Bank of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> The censure was a political maneuver spearheaded by Jackson-rival Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, which served only to perpetuate the animosity between him and Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-brands_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brands-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> During the proceedings preceding the censure, Jackson called Clay "reckless and as full of fury as a drunken man in a brothel", and the issue was highly divisive within the Senate, however the censure was approved 26–20 on March 28.<sup id="cite_ref-brands_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brands-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> When the Jacksonians had a majority in the Senate, the censure was expunged after years of effort by Jackson supporters, led by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)" class="mw-redirect">Thomas Hart Benton</a>, who though he had once shot Jackson in a street fight, eventually became an ardent supporter of the president.<sup id="cite_ref-brands_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brands-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_and_assassination_attempt">Attack and assassination attempt</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/220px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/330px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg/440px-JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1221" data-file-height="843" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JacksonAssassinationAttempt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_Lawrence_(failed_assassin)" title="Richard Lawrence (failed assassin)">Richard Lawrence</a>'s attempt on Jackson's life, as depicted in an 1835 etching.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first recorded physical attack on a U.S. president was directed at Jackson. Jackson had ordered the dismissal of Robert B. Randolph from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">navy</a> for <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzlement</a>. On May 6, 1833, Jackson sailed on USS <i>Cygnet</i> to <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a>, where he was to lay the cornerstone on a monument near the grave of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ball_Washington" title="Mary Ball Washington">Mary Ball Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>'s mother. During a stopover near <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria</a>, Randolph appeared and struck the President. He fled the scene chased by several members of Jackson's party, including the well-known writer <a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>. Jackson decided not to press charges.<sup id="cite_ref-Paletta_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paletta-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On January 30, 1835, what is believed to be the first attempt to kill a sitting President of the United States occurred just outside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>. When Jackson was leaving through the East Portico after the funeral of <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> Representative <a href="/wiki/Warren_R._Davis" title="Warren R. Davis">Warren R. Davis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lawrence_(failed_assassin)" title="Richard Lawrence (failed assassin)">Richard Lawrence</a>, an unemployed house painter from England, aimed a pistol at Jackson, which misfired. Lawrence pulled out a second pistol, which also misfired. Historians believe the humid weather contributed to the double misfiring.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanHeritage.com_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanHeritage.com-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> Lawrence was restrained, and legend says that Jackson attacked Lawrence with his cane. Others present, including <a href="/wiki/Davy_Crockett" title="Davy Crockett">David Crockett</a>, restrained and disarmed Lawrence.</p>
<p>Lawrence told doctors later his reasons for the shooting. He blamed Jackson for the loss of his job. He claimed that with the President dead, "money would be more plenty" (a reference to Jackson's struggle with the Bank of the United States) and that he "could not rise until the President fell". Finally, he told his interrogators that he was a deposed English King—specifically, <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard III</a>, dead since 1485—and that Jackson was his clerk. He was deemed insane and was institutionalized.</p>
<p>Afterwards, due to public curiosity concerning the double misfires, the pistols were tested and retested. Each time they performed perfectly. Many believed that Jackson had been protected by the same <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">Providence</a> that they believed also protected their young nation. The incident became a part of the Jacksonian mythos.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_controversies">Slavery controversies</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-slavery_tracts">Anti-slavery tracts</span></h4>
<p>During the summer of 1835, controversy over slavery was rekindled throughout the nation, as had similarly taken place during the divisive 1819–1820 <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> debates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Northern abolitionists were sending anti-slavery tracts through the U.S. Postal system into the South.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Pro slavery Southerners objected believing the tracts were "incendiary literature" and demanded that the postal service unconditionally ban the sending of any anti-slavery tracts into the South. On July 29, a mob of 300 conservatives broke into the Post Office in Charleston, South Carolina and proceeded to seize and destroy abolitionist tracts. The Jackson Administration had Southern sympathies over slavery and was hostile to abolitionism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Jackson, who demanded sectional peace, desired to placate Southerners; at the same time resisting antislavery demands without ignoring the interests of Northern Democrats. Jackson's Postmaster General <a href="/wiki/Amos_Kendall" title="Amos Kendall">Amos Kendall</a> gave Southern postmasters discretionary powers to either send or detain the anti-slavery tracts. Jackson angrily denounced Northern abolitionists and suggested that the names of abolitionist authors should be published. Jackson, who wanted the matter quickly resolved, also suggested the tracts be mailed only to subscribers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1836, Senator <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, Jackson's former Vice President, authored a bill that would prohibit the sending of any anti-slavery tracts via the federal mail service. The bill however failed to gain enough votes to pass in the House. Many Southern postmasters, however, disregarded matters of federal law and simply refused to send the anti-slavery tracts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002117-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-slavery_Congressional_petitions">Anti-slavery Congressional petitions</span></h4>
<p>In the same year another controversy took place, when abolitionists sent the U.S. House of Representatives petitions to end the slave trade and slavery in Washington, D.C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> This infuriated pro-slavery Southerners, who attempted to prevent acknowledgement or discussion of the petitions. On December 18, 1835 South Carolina congressman <a href="/wiki/James_Henry_Hammond" title="James Henry Hammond">James H. Hammond</a> strongly denounced abolitionists as "ignorant fanatics". Northern Whigs objected that anti-slavery petitions were constitutional and should not be forbidden. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson wanted the issue of these petitions resolved quickly. South Carolina Representative <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Pinckney" title="Henry L. Pinckney">Henry L. Pinckney</a> drafted and introduced a resolution that denounced the petitions as "sickly sentimentality", declared that Congress had no right to interfere with slavery, and tabled (<i><a href="/wiki/Gag_rule" title="Gag rule">gag rule</a></i>) all further anti-slavery petitions. Jackson leaders in Congress supported the measure, which was passed quickly and without any debate; temporarily suppressing pro-abolitionist activities in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002118-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_of_Republic_of_Texas">Recognition of Republic of Texas</span></h4>
<p>In 1835, pro-slavery American settlers in Texas fought the Mexican government; by May 1836, they had routed the Mexican military for the time being, establishing an independent <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Texas" title="Republic of Texas">Republic of Texas</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The new Texas government legalized slavery and demanded recognition from President Jackson and annexation into the United States. However, Jackson was hesitant with recognizing Texas, unconvinced that the new republic could maintain independence from Mexico, and not wanting to make Texas an anti-slavery issue during the 1836 election. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> The strategy worked; the Democratic Party and national loyalties were held intact, while Democratic candidate Van Buren was elected President. Jackson formally recognized the Republic of Texas, nominating a <a href="/wiki/Charg%C3%A9_d%27affaires" title="Chargé d'affaires">chargé d'affaires</a> on the last day of his Presidency, March 3, 1837. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120_91-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002120-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Exploring_Expedition">U.S. Exploring Expedition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Porpoise_(1836).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/220px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/330px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg/440px-USS_Porpoise_%281836%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="545" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Porpoise_(1836).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/USS_Porpoise_(1836)" title="USS Porpoise (1836)">USS&#160;<i>Porpoise</i></a>, a <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> ship laid down in 1835 and launched in May 1836 by Secretary of Navy Dickerson; used in the U.S. Exploring Expedition.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson initially opposed any federal exploration scientific expeditions during his first term in office. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> The last scientific federally funded exploration expeditions took place from 1817 to 1823 led by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Harriman_Long" title="Stephen Harriman Long">Stephen H. Harriman</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red River of the North</a>. Jackson's predecessor <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Q. Adams</a> attempted to launch a scientific oceanic exploration expedition in 1828, but Congress was unwilling to fund the effort. When Jackson assumed office in 1829 he pocketed Adam's expedition plans. However, wanting to establish his presidential legacy, similar to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>, Jackson finally sponsored scientific exploration during his second term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> On May 18, 1836 Jackson signed a law creating and funding the oceanic <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a>. Jackson put Secretary of Navy <a href="/wiki/Mahlon_Dickerson" title="Mahlon Dickerson">Mahlon Dickerson</a> in charge, to assemble suitable ships, officers, and scientific staff for the expedition; with a planned launch before Jackson's term of office expired. Dickerson however proved unfit for the task, preparations stalled and the expedition was not launched until 1838, under the next President, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills_2003705-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> One <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a> ship, <a href="/wiki/USS_Porpoise_(1836)" title="USS Porpoise (1836)">USS&#160;<i>Porpoise</i></a>, later used in the expedition; having been laid down, built, and commissioned by Secretary Dickerson in May 1836, circumnavigated the world, explored and mapped the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>, confirming the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> continent.<sup id="cite_ref-USSPO_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USSPO-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_of_1837">Panic of 1837</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_times_panic_1837.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/220px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/330px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_times_panic_1837.jpg/440px-The_times_panic_1837.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5255" data-file-height="3639" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_times_panic_1837.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A New York newspaper blamed the Panic of 1837 on Andrew Jackson who is depicted in spectacles and top hat</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The national economy during the 1830s was booming and the federal government through duty revenues and sale of public lands was able to pay all bills. In January 1835, Jackson paid off the entire national debt, the only time in U.S. history that has been accomplished.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> However, reckless speculation in land and railroads caused what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Contributing factors included Jackson's veto of the Second National Bank renewal charter in 1832 and subsequent transfer of federal monies to state banks in 1833 that caused Western Banks to relax their lending standards. Two other Jacksonian acts in 1836 contributed to the Panic of 1837, the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Circular" title="Specie Circular">Specie Circular</a>, that mandated Western lands only be purchased by money backed by gold and silver, and the Deposit and Distribution Act, that transferred federal monies from Eastern to western state banks which in turn led to a speculation frenzy by banks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's <i>Specie Circular</i>, although designed to reduce speculation and stabilize the economy, led many investors unable to afford to pay loans backed by gold and silver. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> The same year there was a downturn in Great Britain's economy that stopped investment in the United States. As a result, the U.S. economy went into a depression, banks became insolvent, the national debt (previously paid off) increased, business failures rose, cotton prices dropped, and unemployment dramatically increased.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlson_2002190-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> The depression that followed lasted for four years until 1841 when the economy began to rebound.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_jackson_head.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Andrew_jackson_head.jpg/220px-Andrew_jackson_head.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Andrew_jackson_head.jpg/330px-Andrew_jackson_head.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Andrew_jackson_head.jpg/440px-Andrew_jackson_head.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2472" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_jackson_head.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Andrew Jackson<br />
Official White House Portrait<br />
Ralph E.W. Earl (1835)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Andrew_Jackson" title="Presidency of Andrew Jackson">Presidency of Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_Andrew_Jackson" title="List of federal judges appointed by Andrew Jackson">List of federal judges appointed by Andrew Jackson</a></div>
<p>In total Jackson appointed 24 federal judges: six Justices to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> and eighteen judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district courts</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>&#160;– June 15, 1836.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>&#160;– January 26, 1837.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/220px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" width="220" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/330px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg/440px-78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="681" data-file-height="932" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:78yo_Andrew_Jackson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photographic copy of an 1845 <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After serving two terms as president, Jackson retired to his <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">Hermitage plantation</a> in 1837.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> He immediately began putting the Hermitage in order as it had been poorly managed in his absence by his adopted son, Andrew Jr. Although he suffered ill health, Jackson remained influential in both national and state politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a firm advocate of the federal union of the states and rejected any talk of secession, insisting, "I will die with the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> Blamed for causing the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>, he was unpopular in his early retirement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson continued to denounce the "perfidy and treachery" of banks and urged his successor, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> to repudiate the <i>Specie Circular</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELatner_2002121-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson died at his plantation on June 8, 1845, at the age of 78, of chronic <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dropsy" title="Dropsy" class="mw-redirect">dropsy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>. According to a newspaper account from the <i>Boon Lick Times</i> read, "[he] fainted whilst being removed from his chair to the bed ... but he subsequently revived ... Gen. Jackson died at the Hermitage at 6&#160;o'clock P.M. on Sunday the 8th instant. When the messenger finally came, the old soldier, patriot and Christian was looking out for his approach. He is gone, but his memory lives, and will continue to live."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his will, Jackson left his entire estate to his adopted son, Andrew Jackson Jr., except for specifically enumerated items that were left to various other friends and family members.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_personal_life">Family and personal life</span></h2>
<p>Shortly after Jackson first arrived in Nashville in 1788, he lived as a boarder with Rachel Stockley Donelson, the widow of <a href="/wiki/John_Donelson" title="John Donelson">John Donelson</a>. Here Jackson became acquainted with their daughter, <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Jackson" title="Rachel Jackson">Rachel Donelson Robards</a>. At the time, Rachel was in an unhappy marriage with Captain Lewis Robards; he was subject to fits of jealous rage.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> The two were separated in 1790. According to Jackson, he married Rachel after hearing that Robards had obtained a divorce. However, the divorce had never been completed, making Rachel's marriage to Jackson bigamous and therefore invalid. After the divorce was officially completed, Rachel and Jackson remarried in 1794.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> To complicate matters further, evidence shows that Rachel had been living with Jackson and referred to herself as Mrs. Jackson before the petition for divorce was ever made.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> It was not uncommon on the frontier for relationships to be formed and dissolved unofficially, as long as they were recognized by the community.</p>
<p>The controversy surrounding their marriage remained a sore point for Jackson, who deeply resented attacks on his wife's honor. By May 1806, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickinson_(historical_figure)" title="Charles Dickinson (historical figure)">Charles Dickinson</a> had published an attack on Jackson in the local newspaper, and it resulted in a written challenge from Jackson to a <a href="/wiki/Duel" title="Duel">duel</a>. Since Dickinson was considered an expert shot, Jackson determined it would be best to let Dickinson turn and fire first, hoping that his aim might be spoiled in his quickness; Jackson would wait and take careful aim at Dickinson. Dickinson did fire first, hitting Jackson in the chest. The bullet that struck Jackson was so close to his heart that it was never safely removed. Under the rules of dueling, Dickinson had to remain still as Jackson took aim and shot and killed him. Jackson's behavior in the duel outraged men of honor in Tennessee, who called it a brutal, cold-blooded killing and saddled Jackson with a reputation as a violent, vengeful man. As a result, he became a social outcast.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Rachel died of a heart attack on December 22, 1828, two weeks after her husband's victory in the election and two months before Jackson took office as President. Jackson described her symptoms as "excruciating pain in the left shoulder, arm, and breast."<sup id="cite_ref-Brands_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brands-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> After struggling for three days, Rachel finally died; a distraught Jackson had to be pulled from her so the undertaker could prepare the body.<sup id="cite_ref-Brands_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brands-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> She had been under extreme stress during the election, and she never did well when Jackson was away at war or work. Jackson blamed <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> for Rachel's death because the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a> campaign of 1828 had repeatedly attacked the circumstances for Jackson's wedding to Rachel. He felt that this had hastened her death and never forgave Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Jackson had three adopted sons: Theodore, an Indian about whom little is known,<sup id="cite_ref-Theodore_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Andrew Jackson Jr., the son of Rachel's brother Severn Donelson, and Lyncoya, a Creek Indian orphan adopted by Jackson after the Creek War. Lyncoya died of tuberculosis in 1828, at the age of sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Jacksons also acted as guardians for eight other children. John Samuel Donelson, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Smith_Donelson" title="Daniel Smith Donelson">Daniel Smith Donelson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Donelson" title="Andrew Jackson Donelson">Andrew Jackson Donelson</a> were the sons of Rachel's brother Samuel Donelson, who died in 1804. Andrew Jackson Hutchings was Rachel's orphaned grand nephew. Caroline Butler, Eliza Butler, Edward Butler, and Anthony Butler were the orphaned children of Edward Butler, a family friend. They came to live with the Jacksons after the death of their father.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The widower Jackson invited Rachel's niece <a href="/wiki/Emily_Donelson" title="Emily Donelson">Emily Donelson</a> to serve as hostess at the White House. Emily was married to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Donelson" title="Andrew Jackson Donelson">Andrew Jackson Donelson</a>, who acted as Jackson's private secretary and in 1856 would run for Vice President on the <a href="/wiki/Know_Nothing" title="Know Nothing">American Party</a> ticket. The relationship between the President and Emily became strained during the <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Petticoat affair</a>, and the two became estranged for over a year. They eventually reconciled and she resumed her duties as White House hostess. <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Yorke_Jackson" title="Sarah Yorke Jackson">Sarah Yorke Jackson</a>, the wife of Andrew Jackson Jr., became co-hostess of the White House in 1834. It was the only time in history when two women simultaneously acted as unofficial First Lady. Sarah took over all hostess duties after Emily died from tuberculosis in 1836. Jackson used <a href="/wiki/Rip_Raps" title="Rip Raps">Rip Raps</a> as a retreat, visiting between August 19, 1829 through August 16, 1835.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperament">Temperament</span></h3>
<p>Jackson's quick temper was notorious. Brands says, "His audacity on behalf of the people earned him enemies who slandered him and defamed even his wife, Rachel. He dueled in her defense and his own, suffering grievous wounds that left him with bullet fragments lodged about his body."<sup id="cite_ref-Brands_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brands-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Remini is of the opinion that Jackson was often in control of his rage, and used it (and his fearsome reputation) as a tool to get what he wanted in his public and private affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Brands also notes that his opponents were terrified of his temper:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Observers likened him to a volcano, and only the most intrepid or recklessly curious cared to see it erupt.... His close associates all had stories of his blood-curling oaths, his summoning of the Almighty to loose His wrath upon some miscreant, typically followed by his own vow to hang the villain or blow him to perdition. Given his record – in duels, brawls, mutiny trials, and summary hearings – listeners had to take his vows seriously.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>On the last day of the presidency, Jackson admitted that he had but two regrets, that he "had been unable to shoot Henry Clay or to hang John C. Calhoun."<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/220px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/330px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg/440px-Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="931" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tennessee_Gentleman_portrait_of_Andrew_Jackson_by_Ralph_E._W._Earl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Tennessee Gentleman</i>, portrait of Jackson, c. 1831, from the collection of <a href="/wiki/The_Hermitage_(Nashville,_Tennessee)" title="The Hermitage (Nashville, Tennessee)">The Hermitage</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Jackson was a lean figure, standing at 6 feet, 1 inch (1.85&#160;m) tall, and weighing between 130 and 140 pounds (64&#160;kg) on average. Jackson also had an unruly shock of red hair, which had completely grayed by the time he became president at age 61. He had penetrating deep blue eyes. Jackson was one of the more sickly presidents, suffering from chronic headaches, abdominal pains, and a hacking cough, caused by a musket ball in his lung that was never removed, that often brought up blood and sometimes made his whole body shake.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_faith">Religious faith</span></h3>
<p>About a year after retiring the presidency,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Presbyterian_Church,_Nashville" title="Downtown Presbyterian Church, Nashville" class="mw-redirect">First Presbyterian Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>.</p>
<p>Jackson was a Freemason, having been initiated at Harmony Lodge No. 1 in Tennessee; he also participated in chartering several other lodges in Tennessee. He was the only U.S. president to have served as Grand Master of a state's Grand Lodge until <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> in 1945. His Masonic apron is on display in the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Museum" title="Tennessee State Museum">Tennessee State Museum</a>. An obelisk and bronze Masonic plaque decorate his tomb at The Hermitage.<sup id="cite_ref-Tennessee_State_History_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tennessee_State_History-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GLBC.26Y_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLBC.26Y-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span></h2>
<p>Jackson remains one of the most studied and most controversial Americans of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian Charles Sellers says "Andrew Jackson's masterful personality was enough by itself to make him one of the most controversial figures ever to stride across the American stage."<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> His most controversial presidential actions included removal of the Indians from the southeast, the dismantling of the Bank of the United States, and his threat to use military force against the state of South Carolina to make it stop nullifying federal laws. Not at all controversial was his great victory over the British at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_New_Orleans" title="Battle of New Orleans">New Orleans</a> in the last battle of the War of 1812. He was the main founder of the modern Democratic Party and remains its iconic hero; he was always a fierce partisan, with many friends and many enemies.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jackson_on_U.S._postage">Jackson on U.S. postage</span></h3>
<p>Andrew Jackson is one of the few American presidents ever to appear on U.S. Postage more than the usual two or three times. He died in 1845, but the U.S. Post Office did not release a <a href="/wiki/U.S._Presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps" title="U.S. Presidents on U.S. postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">postage stamp</a> in his honor until 18 years after his death, with the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Presidents_on_U.S._Postage_stamps#First_appearances" title="U.S. Presidents on U.S. Postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">issue of 1863</a>, a 2-cent black issue, commonly referred to by collectors as the '<a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(stamp)" title="Black Jack (stamp)">Black Jack</a>'. In contrast, the first <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> stamp was released only one month after his death, Lincoln, one year exactly. As Jackson was a controversial figure in his day there is speculation that officials in Washington chose to wait a period of time before issuing a stamp with his portrait. In all, Jackson has appeared on <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps#Andrew_Jackson" title="U.S. presidents on U.S. postage stamps">thirteen different U.S. postage stamps</a>, more than that of most U.S. presidents; only Washington, Lincoln, and Franklin (who was not a president) have appeared more often.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> During the American Civil War the Confederate government also issued two <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_Confederate_States" title="Postage stamps and postal history of the Confederate States">Confederate postage stamps</a> bearing Jackson's portrait, one a <a href="/wiki/File:Red_Jack-2c.jpg" title="File:Red Jack-2c.jpg">2-cent red stamp</a> and the other a <a href="/wiki/File:Csa_jackson_1862-2c.jpg" title="File:Csa jackson 1862-2c.jpg">2-cent green stamp</a>, both issued in 1863.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
