<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_$20_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/220px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/330px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg/440px-US_%2420_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7326" data-file-height="3128" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_$20_Series_2006_Obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Andrew Jackson portrait on obverse $20.00 bill circa 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Jackson's portrait appears on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty-dollar_bill" title="United States twenty-dollar bill">United States twenty-dollar bill</a>. He has appeared on $5, $10, $50, and $10,000 bills in the past, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> $1,000 bill.</li>
<li>Jackson's image is on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Jack_(stamp)" title="Black Jack (stamp)">Black Jack</a> and many other <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamps</a>. These include the <a href="/wiki/Prominent_Americans_series" title="Prominent Americans series">Prominent Americans series</a> (1965–1978) 10¢ stamp.</li>
<li>Numerous counties and cities are named after him, including the city of Jacksonville in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_North_Carolina" title="Jacksonville, North Carolina">North Carolina</a>; the city of Jackson in <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Louisiana" title="Jackson, Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Michigan" title="Jackson, Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Jackson, Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Missouri" title="Jackson, Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Tennessee" title="Jackson, Tennessee">Tennessee</a>; Jackson County in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Florida" title="Jackson County, Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Illinois" title="Jackson County, Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Mississippi" title="Jackson County, Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="Jackson County, Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Ohio" title="Jackson County, Ohio">Ohio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Oregon" title="Jackson County, Oregon">Oregon</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Jackson Parish, Louisiana">Jackson Parish</a> in Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Memorials to Jackson include a set of four identical equestrian statues by the sculptor <a href="/wiki/Clark_Mills_(sculptor)" title="Clark Mills (sculptor)">Clark Mills</a>: in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Square,_New_Orleans" title="Jackson Square, New Orleans" class="mw-redirect">Jackson Square</a> in New Orleans; in Nashville on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_State_Capitol" title="Tennessee State Capitol">Tennessee State Capitol</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> near the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>; and in <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a>. Other equestrian statues of Jackson have been erected elsewhere, as in the State Capitol grounds in <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_State_Park" title="Andrew Jackson State Park">Andrew Jackson State Park</a> is located on the site of his birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory_Boulevard" title="Old Hickory Boulevard">Old Hickory Boulevard</a> in Nashville is named for him.</li>
<li>Two suburbs in the eastern part of Nashville are named in honor of Jackson and his home: <a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory,_Tennessee" title="Old Hickory, Tennessee">Old Hickory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermitage,_Tennessee" title="Hermitage, Tennessee">Hermitage</a>.</li>
<li>A main thoroughfare in <a href="/wiki/Hermitage,_Tennessee" title="Hermitage, Tennessee">Hermitage</a> is named Andrew Jackson Parkway. Several roads in the same area have names associated with Jackson, such as Andrew Jackson Way, Andrew Jackson Place, Rachel Donelson Pass, Rachel's Square Drive, Rachel's Way, Rachel's Court, Rachel's Trail, and Andrew Donelson Drive.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Hickory_Lake" title="Old Hickory Lake">Old Hickory Lake</a> is located in north central Tennessee.</li>
<li>Andrew Jackson High School, in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_South_Carolina" title="Lancaster County, South Carolina">Lancaster County, South Carolina</a>, is named after him and uses the title of "Hickory Log" for its Annual photo book.</li>
<li>The section of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_74" title="U.S. Route 74">U.S. Route 74</a> between <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_North_Carolina" title="Wilmington, North Carolina">Wilmington, North Carolina</a> is named the Andrew Jackson Highway.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fort_Jackson_(South_Carolina)" title="Fort Jackson (South Carolina)">Fort Jackson</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia, South Carolina</a>, is named in his honor.</li>
<li>Fort Jackson, built before the Civil War on the Mississippi River for the defense of New Orleans, was named in his honor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/USS_Andrew_Jackson_(SSBN-619)" title="USS Andrew Jackson (SSBN-619)">USS&#160;<i>Andrew Jackson</i>&#160;(SSBN-619)</a>, a <i>Lafayette</i>-class nuclear-powered ballistic missile submarine, which served from 1963 to 1989.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jackson_Park_(Chicago)" title="Jackson Park (Chicago)">Jackson Park</a>, the third-largest park in Chicago, is named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jackson_Park_(Seattle)" title="Jackson Park (Seattle)">Jackson Park</a>, a public golf course in <a href="/wiki/Seattle,_Washington" title="Seattle, Washington" class="mw-redirect">Seattle, Washington</a>, is named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Centre" title="Andrew Jackson Centre">Andrew Jackson Centre</a>, the <i>Andrew Jackson Cottage and US Rangers Centre</i> in Northern Ireland, is a "traditional thatched Ulster–Scots farmhouse built in 1750s" and includes the home of Jackson's parents", which has been restored.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrick_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrick-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Andrew Jackson Masonic Lodge No. 120, in the Jurisdiction of Virginia, is named for him.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_culture_depictions">Popular culture depictions</span></h3>
<p>The actor Hugh Sothern played the character of General Andrew Jackson in the 1938 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Buccaneer_(1938_film)" title="The Buccaneer (1938 film)">The Buccaneer</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> played Andrew Jackson in the 1953 film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_President%27s_Lady" title="The President's Lady">The President's Lady</a></i> and the 1958 film, <a href="/wiki/The_Buccaneer_(1958_film)" title="The Buccaneer (1958 film)"><i>The Buccaneer</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Basil_Ruysdael" title="Basil Ruysdael">Basil Ruysdael</a> played Jackson in <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>'s 1955 <a href="/wiki/Davy_Crockett_(TV_miniseries)" title="Davy Crockett (TV miniseries)" class="mw-redirect"><i>Davy Crockett</i> TV miniseries</a> and subsequent film release.</p>

<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the antebellum South, rural schools were often built in exhausted cotton or tobacco fields, hence the name.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Paletta-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Paletta_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Paletta_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Paletta, Lu Ann; Worth, Fred L (1988). <i>The World Almanac of Presidential Facts</i>. <li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Ostermeier, Eric (December 4, 2013). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://blog.lib.umn.edu/cspg/smartpolitics/2013/12/bob_smith_and_the_12-year_itch.php">"Bob Smith and the 12-Year Itch"</a>. <i>Smart Politics</i>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndrew+Jackson&amp;rft.aufirst=Eric&amp;rft.aulast=Ostermeier&amp;rft.btitle=Bob+Smith+and+the+12-Year+Itch&amp;rft.date=December+4%2C+2013&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fblog.lib.umn.edu%2Fcspg%2Fsmartpolitics%2F2013%2F12%2Fbob_smith_and_the_12-year_itch.php&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bioguide.congress.gov/scripts/biodisplay.pl?index=j000005">Andrew Jackson</a> at the <i><a href="/wiki/Biographical_Directory_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Biographical Directory of the United States Congress">Biographical Directory of the United States Congress</a></i>. Retrieved October 30, 2011</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Walter T. Durham, <i>Before Tennessee: The Southwest Territory</i>; Piney Flats, TN: Rocky Mount Historical Association, 1990; pp. 218–19. "In the Mero District, two young attorneys, John Overton and Andrew Jackson, entered into a formal partnership on May 12, 1794, 'for the purpose of purchasing lands as well as those lands without as within the military bounds.' Theirs was a frank avowal; they, like many of their contemporaries, would deal with lands within Indian territory. Most of the transactions involved grants made under the 'land grab' act of 1873 that briefly opened to claim by North Carolinians all of the Indian lands in that state's transmontane west. While the act was in force, citizens had staked claims to two or three million acres of Chickasaw and Cherokee land."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jackson historian Steve Inskeep reports: Recent Jackson biographers, such as <a href="/wiki/Jon_Meacham" title="Jon Meacham">Jon Meacham</a> and <a href="/wiki/H.W._Brands" title="H.W. <li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.pbs.org/kcet/andrewjackson/features/the_spoils_system.html">"The Power of the Presidency: The Spoils System"</a>. <i>Andrew Jackson – The Good, Evil &amp; The Presidency – Special Features – PBS.org</i>. Red Hill Productions and Community Television of Southern California<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 16,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAndrew+Jackson&amp;rft.btitle=The+Power+of+the+Presidency%3A+The+Spoils+System&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pbs.org%2Fkcet%2Fandrewjackson%2Ffeatures%2Fthe_spoils_system.html&amp;rft.pub=Red+Hill+Productions+and+Community+Television+of+Southern+California&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">Spoils system</a>, as the rotation in office system was called, did not originate with Jackson. It originated with New York governors in the late 18th and early 19th centuries (most notably <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a> and <a href="/wiki/DeWitt_Clinton" title="DeWitt Clinton">DeWitt Clinton</a>). <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> brought it to the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Branch" title="Executive Branch" class="mw-redirect">Executive Branch</a> when he replaced Federalist office-holders after becoming President. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.u-s-history.com/pages/h965.html">The Spoils System versus the Merit System.</a> Retrieved on November 21, 2006.</span></li>
